Second Proof: Non-Being Cannot Be Part of the Cause
Al-Razi presents a dilemma based on Ibn Sina's view of divine essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's second proof against Ibn Sina's view that God's essence is merely existence with a negative qualification, and why non-being cannot be part of a cause.

Core Idea
Non-being cannot be part of a cause of existence.

Al-Razi continues his refutation of Ibn Sina's conception of God as existence with a negative qualification. This second proof exposes a fatal flaw: if the negative qualification (a non-being) were part of the divine essence that causes contingent existences, then non-being would be part of the cause of existence — an absurdity that would undermine all cosmological reasoning. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: a conditional statement followed by two possibilities.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the core argument is complete.
2
Understand the dilemma
Identify the two cases: either the origin of contingent existences is that existence without sharing the negation, or with sharing the negation.
3
Analyze the first case
If the origin is existence alone without the negation, then the latest existent would share in origination with God — that is problematic.
4
Analyze the second case
If the origin includes the negation, then non-being becomes part of the cause, which is impossible. Understand why this is absurd.
If non-being could be part of a cause, it could be the complete cause, undermining all proofs from existence.
5
Reflect on the argument's implication
Consider how this argument refutes Ibn Sina's view and why it matters for the proof of God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على فساد هذا المذهب : أنه لو لم تكن للباري تعالى ماهية وحقيقة إلا الوجود المقيد بالقيد السلبي وهو أنه غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات فمبدأ وجودات الممكنات إما أن يكون هو ذلك الوجود لا بشركة من ذلك السلب وإما أن يكون بشركة من ذلك السلب، فإن المبدأ هو ذلك الوجود لا بمشاركة من ذلك السلب وجب أن يكون أخر الموجودات مشاركًا لذات الحق سبحانه وتعالى في تلك المبدئية، وإن كانت المبدئية بمشاركة من ذلك السلب كان السلب جزءا من مبدأ الثبوت، وذلك محال ؛ إذ لو جاز في العقل أن يكون العدم جزءا لعلة الثبوت فليتجه أيضًا أن يكون تمام علة الثبوت، وحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نستدل بوجود الممكنات
The second proof of the falsity of this doctrine: that if the Creator, exalted, had no essence and reality except existence restricted by the negative qualification, namely that it is not accidental to anything of the essences, then the origin of the existences of possibles is either that existence without sharing that negation, or with sharing that negation. If the origin is that existence without participation of that negation, then the latest of existents must share with the essence of the Truth, glory be to Him and exalted, in that origination. And if the origination is with participation of that negation, then the negation becomes a part of the cause of existence, which is impossible; for if it were intellectually permissible for non-existence to be part of the cause of existence, it would also be directed that it be the complete cause of existence, and then we would not be able to infer from the existence of possibles.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof against Ibn Sina's view that God's essence is only existence with a negative qualification (i.e., not accidental to any essence). Al-Razi sets up the argument: if God's essence is just that, then the origin (mabda') of contingent existences is either that existence alone (without including the negation) or that existence together with the negation. In the first case, the most recent of existents would share in that origination with God, which is absurd. In the second case, the negation (which is a non-being) becomes part of the cause of existence. But it is impossible for non-being to be part of a cause of existence, because if non-being could be part of a cause, it could also be the complete cause, which would undermine all cosmological arguments. The proof is cut off, but the reasoning is clear: the negative qualification cannot play a causal role in bringing about contingent things.

Key Terms
وجود مقيد بالقيد السلبي
مبدأ
سلب
علة الثبوت
عدم
مشاركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a negative qualification (like 'not accidental') is harmless and does not affect the causal role of God's essence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if the negative qualification is part of God's essence, then when that essence causes existence, the negation (a non-being) becomes part of the cause, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if the origination of contingent existences involves the negative qualification as part of the cause, what would be the consequence?
A) God would be identical to His essence
B) Non-being would be part of the cause of existence, which is impossible
C) The argument becomes trivial
D) Existence would be accidental to essences

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's second proof demonstrates that if the negative qualification were part of God's essence as Ibn Sina claims, it would lead to the absurdity of non-being being part of the cause of existence, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-being cannot be part of a cause, what does this imply about the absolute perfection of the Creator? Does your heart truly accept that God's essence is utterly simple and self-sufficient, or do you still mentally attach negative qualifications as if they are harmless?
?
Al-Razi's argument shows that if non-being could be part of a cause, all proofs from effects would collapse. Are you personally basing your faith on such rational arguments? If these arguments were refuted, would your certainty remain? Examine your reliance on logic.
?
This dense logical reasoning aims to guard God's transcendence. When you study such arguments, do you feel your iman strengthened, or do you find yourself merely satisfying intellectual curiosity? Ask yourself: Has this lesson moved you to greater awe of Allah, or is it just an academic exercise?

The Trilemma of Existence
Razi presents a trilemma on existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that God's existence cannot be pure existence without being accidental to an essence, by showing that each possible relation between existence and essence leads to an absurdity.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be neutral; it forces either accident or essence-identity, leading to contradictions.

Razi presents a powerful trilemma against Ibn Sina's view that God's existence is pure. He shows that existence, taken in itself, must either necessitate being accidental to an essence, necessitate being non-accidental, or necessitate neither. Each option leads to an absurdity, thereby refuting the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the three conditional statements introduced by 'فإن اقتضى', 'وما إن اقتضى', and 'أما إن قلنا'.
These correspond to the three branches of the trilemma.
2
Analyze each branch
For each branch, identify the consequence that Razi draws and why it is unacceptable. The first leads to God's existence being accidental to an essence. The second leads to either non-existence of contingents or homonymy of 'existence'. The third leads to God being dependent on another.
Focus on the logical flow: if P then Q, but Q is false, so P is false.
3
Understand the third branch in depth
The third branch is the most subtle: if existence is neutral, then a separate cause is needed to make God's existence pure, making God dependent—a contradiction with 'necessary being'.
This closes all apparent loopholes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو لا يقتضي لا أن يكون عارضًا ولا أن يكون غير عارض، بل الأمران جائزان عليه، فإن اقتضى الوجود من حيث إنه وجود أن يكون عارضًا لماهية من الماهيات وجب أن يكون وجود واجب الوجود عارضًا لماهية من الماهيات؛ فقوله : إنه وجود مجرد غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات قول باطل وما إن اقتضى الوجود أن يكون غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات وجب أن لا يكون شيء من الوجودات عارضًا لشيء من الماهيات ،البتة فهذه الممكنات إما أن لا تكون موجودة أصلا وإن كانت موجودة كانت وجوداتها نفس ماهياتها، فيكون لفظ الوجود واقعا على الموجودات بالاشتراك اللفظي لا بالاشتراك المعنوي، وقد بينا أن ذلك باطل وأن هذا البحث متفرع على أن لفظ الموجود واقع على الواجب والممكن بالاشتراك المعنوي. وأما إن قلنا الوجود من حيث إنه وجود لا يقتضي أن يكون عارضًا لماهية ولا أن يكون غير عارض لها، فحينئذ يمتنع أن يصير عارضًا أو غير عارض إلا لسبب منفصل، فوجود واجب الوجود لا يصير مجردًا إلا بسبب منفصل فيكون واجب الوجود لذاته واجب الوجود لغيره، وهو محال. وأيضا : قد عرفت أن الماهيات المتساوية في تمام تلك الماهية كل ما على كل واحد منها صح على كلها، فإذن كل ما صح على جميع الموجودات العارضة لماهيات الممكنات وجب أن يصح على واجب الوجود، وكل ما ثبت لواجب الوجود وجب أن يثبت لوجودات جميع الماهيات الممكنات، وكل ذلك باطل قطعاً، ولما ثبت بهذا البرهان القطعي امتناع هذه الأقسام، ثبت أن القول الذي اختاره أبو علي بن سينا قول مردود.
Or it does not necessitate that it be an accident and does not necessitate that it be not an accident, rather both possibilities are permissible for it. For if existence, insofar as it is existence, necessitates that it be an accident to some essence, then the existence of the Necessary Being must be an accident to some essence; so his statement that 'It is pure existence, not accidental to any essence' is a false statement. And if existence necessitates that it be not an accident to any essence, then none of existents must ever be an accident to any essence. Then these contingents either are not existent at all, or if they are existent, their existences are their essences themselves, so the word 'existence' would apply to existents by verbal homonymy not by conceptual commonality, and we have shown that is false and that this investigation branches from the fact that the word 'existent' applies to the Necessary and the contingent by conceptual commonality. And if we say that existence, insofar as it is existence, does not necessitate that it be an accident to an essence nor that it be not an accident to it, then it is impossible for it to become an accident or not an accident except due to a separate cause. Then the existence of the Necessary Being does not become pure except due to a separate cause, so the Necessary Being in itself would be necessary through another, which is impossible. And also: you have known that essences equal in the entirety of that essence — whatever holds for one of them holds for all. So everything that holds for all existents accidental to contingent essences must hold for the Necessary Being, and everything proven for the Necessary Being must be proven for the existences of all contingent essences. All of that is absolutely false. And when these divisions are proven impossible by this decisive proof, it is established that the view chosen by Abu Ali ibn Sina is a rejected view.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God's existence is pure existence not attaching to any essence. Razi presents a dilemma: either existence by itself necessitates being accidental to an essence, or it necessitates being non-accidental, or it necessitates neither. If the first, then God's existence must be accidental to some essence (contradiction). If the second, then no existence can be accidental, so contingent existences would have to be identical to their essences, leading to homonymy in the term 'existent', which is false. If the third, then a separate cause would be needed for God's existence to be pure, making God dependent (impossible). Then an additional argument is given: whatever holds for all accidental existents should hold for God, and vice versa, leading to absurdities. Thus Ibn Sina's view is rejected.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
عارض
واجب الوجود
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's existence can be 'pure' in the sense that it has no relation to essence, neither accidental nor non-accidental.
Correction: Razi shows that if existence is neutral, then God's purity would depend on an external cause, undermining His necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of the second branch (existence necessitates being non-accidental)?
A) God's existence would be accidental to an essence.
B) None of the existents would be accidental, so contingent existences must be identical to their essences, leading to homonymy.
C) God would be dependent on a separate cause.
D) Existence would not apply to God at all.

Key Takeaway
Razi's trilemma shows that the claim that God's existence is pure and non-accidental to any essence leads to contradictions in every possible scenario.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument shows that any attempt to conceptualize God's existence leads to logical dead ends. How does this challenge your own attempts to 'understand' God? Are you willing to accept that your mind cannot fully comprehend His nature? What would it mean for you to worship a God who is beyond your logical categories?
?
Consider the third horn: if existence is neutral, then God's simplicity would depend on a separate cause. What does this reveal about the limits of philosophical reasoning about God? Does this affect your reliance on rational arguments to affirm your faith? How can you maintain conviction while acknowledging the inadequacy of human concepts?
?
Razi's argument forces you to choose between conceptual consistency and the reality of God. Are you prepared to abandon any neat philosophical account of God's nature? What personal attachments to intellectual comfort must you surrender in light of this trilemma?

The Argument from Commonality of Existence
Razi uses the principle of equal essences to refute Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's additional argument that the univocity of existence (اشتراك معنوي) forces a contradiction if God's existence were pure and non-accidental.

Core Idea
If existence applies equally to all, then properties of contingent existence must apply to God, which is absurd.

After dismantling the direct claim with a trilemma, Razi gives a second argument based on the conceptual commonality of existence. He uses the principle that essences equal in their entirety share all attributes. Since existence is a common concept, whatever holds for contingent existences (being accidental to an essence) must hold for God's existence, and vice versa, leading to absurdities. Thus Ibn Sina's view is decisively refuted.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of equal essences
Razi refers to a known principle: if two essences are equal in every respect, whatever holds for one holds for the other. Here, existence is considered as a common essence (ماهية) shared by all existents.
Remember that 'existence' is taken as a single concept applying to God and creatures (اشتراك معنوي).
2
Apply the principle to existence
If existence is a common concept, then whatever is true for all contingent existences (namely, being accidental to an essence) must be true for God's existence. This would make God's existence accidental, which is false. Conversely, any attribute of God's existence (like being pure) would have to apply to contingent existences, which is also false.
Razi states both directions: what holds for all accidental existents holds for God, and what holds for God holds for all contingent existences.
3
Conclude the refutation
Since both directions lead to absurdities (as they contradict basic theological truths), the premise that God's existence is pure and non-accidental must be rejected. Razi declares Ibn Sina's view 'rejected'.
This argument reinforces the trilemma and provides an independent refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو لا يقتضي لا أن يكون عارضًا ولا أن يكون غير عارض، بل الأمران جائزان عليه، فإن اقتضى الوجود من حيث إنه وجود أن يكون عارضًا لماهية من الماهيات وجب أن يكون وجود واجب الوجود عارضًا لماهية من الماهيات؛ فقوله : إنه وجود مجرد غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات قول باطل وما إن اقتضى الوجود أن يكون غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات وجب أن لا يكون شيء من الوجودات عارضًا لشيء من الماهيات ،البتة فهذه الممكنات إما أن لا تكون موجودة أصلا وإن كانت موجودة كانت وجوداتها نفس ماهياتها، فيكون لفظ الوجود واقعا على الموجودات بالاشتراك اللفظي لا بالاشتراك المعنوي، وقد بينا أن ذلك باطل وأن هذا البحث متفرع على أن لفظ الموجود واقع على الواجب والممكن بالاشتراك المعنوي. وأما إن قلنا الوجود من حيث إنه وجود لا يقتضي أن يكون عارضًا لماهية ولا أن يكون غير عارض لها، فحينئذ يمتنع أن يصير عارضًا أو غير عارض إلا لسبب منفصل، فوجود واجب الوجود لا يصير مجردًا إلا بسبب منفصل فيكون واجب الوجود لذاته واجب الوجود لغيره، وهو محال. وأيضا : قد عرفت أن الماهيات المتساوية في تمام تلك الماهية كل ما على كل واحد منها صح على كلها، فإذن كل ما صح على جميع الموجودات العارضة لماهيات الممكنات وجب أن يصح على واجب الوجود، وكل ما ثبت لواجب الوجود وجب أن يثبت لوجودات جميع الماهيات الممكنات، وكل ذلك باطل قطعاً، ولما ثبت بهذا البرهان القطعي امتناع هذه الأقسام، ثبت أن القول الذي اختاره أبو علي بن سينا قول مردود.
Or it does not necessitate that it be an accident and does not necessitate that it be not an accident, rather both possibilities are permissible for it. For if existence, insofar as it is existence, necessitates that it be an accident to some essence, then the existence of the Necessary Being must be an accident to some essence; so his statement that 'It is pure existence, not accidental to any essence' is a false statement. And if existence necessitates that it be not an accident to any essence, then none of existents must ever be an accident to any essence. What does that do to your prayer and worship?
?

First Absurdity: Other Beings Share in God's Principality
Razi presents the first horn of his argument against the negative qualification view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that if the principle of contingents is existence without the negative qualification, then other existents would share in God's unique role as the principle of existence.

Core Idea
Sharing principality with God is absurd because it implies multiple ultimate causes.

Razi begins his second proof against the view that God's essence is just existence with a negative qualification. He considers the first possibility: that the principle of contingents is that existence without the negation. He shows that this leads to the absurd conclusion that other beings would share in God's principality. This lesson examines that first horn of the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Notice the description of the view being refuted.
The view states God's essence is existence plus a negative qualification (non-accidental).
2
Identify the division
Razi splits the possibilities for the principle of contingents into two: without or with the negation.
3
Analyze the first horn
If the principle is existence without the negation, then any being with existence would have that same principle.
This leads to sharing in principality.
4
Reflect on the absurdity
Consider why sharing principality is problematic: it conflicts with God's uniqueness as the ultimate cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على فساد هذا المذهب : أنه لو لم تكن للباري تعالى ماهية وحقيقة إلا الوجود المقيد بالقيد السلبي وهو أنه غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات فمبدأ وجودات الممكنات إما أن يكون هو ذلك الوجود لا بشركة من ذلك السلب وإما أن يكون بشركة من ذلك السلب، فإن المبدأ هو ذلك الوجود لا بمشاركة من ذلك السلب وجب أن يكون أخر الموجودات مشاركًا لذات الحق سبحانه وتعالى في تلك المبدئية، وإن كانت المبدئية بمشاركة من ذلك السلب كان السلب جزءا من مبدأ الثبوت، وذلك محال ؛ إذ لو جاز في العقل أن يكون العدم جزءا لعلة الثبوت فليتجه أيضًا أن يكون تمام علة الثبوت، وحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نستدل بوجود الممكنات على وجود واجب الوجود. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الوجود المجرد مستلزما لصفة ويكون تلك الصفة مبدأ للممكنات؟ الوجود مع أن قلنا : التقسيم المذكور عائد في كيفية استلزام الوجود لتلك الصفة، وهو المؤثر في ذلك الاستلزام إما الوجود لا بمشاركة ذلك السلب أو بمشاركة ذلك السلب.
The second proof on the corruption of this doctrine: That if the Almighty has no essence and reality except existence restricted by a negative restriction—that it is not accidental to any essence—then the principle of the existences of contingents is either that existence without participation of that negation, or with participation of that negation. If the principle is that existence without participation of that negation, then other existents must share with the essence of the Truth, Exalted, in that principality. And if the principality is with participation of that negation, then negation becomes part of the principle of affirmation, and that is impossible; for if it were rationally possible for non-being to be part of the cause of being, then it would also be possible for it to be the entire cause of being. And then we cannot infer from the existence of contingents the existence of the Necessary Being. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that that pure existence entails a attribute and that attribute is the principle of contingents? (We say:) The aforementioned division returns concerning how existence entails that attribute, and the effective factor in that entailment is either existence without participation of that negation or with participation of that negation.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the view that God's essence is just existence with the negative qualification of being non-accidental. Razi considers the principle of contingent existences: if it is that existence without the negation, then other existents would share in principality (absurd). If the negation is part of the principle, then non-being becomes part of the cause of being, which is rationally impossible and would undermine the cosmological argument. An objection is raised: perhaps the pure existence entails an attribute which is the principle. Razi's answer: the same division applies—the entailment must be either with or without the negation, repeating the problem.

Key Terms
ماهية
قيد سلبي
مبدأ
سلب
مبدئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, what follows if the principle of contingents is existence without the negative qualification?
A) The principle would be impossible.
B) Other existents would share in God's principality.
C) God would have an essence.
D) The negation would be part of the cause.

Key Takeaway
If God's essence is existence without any negative qualification, then any being that exists would share in His role as the principle of existence, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that if God were just existence, then anything that exists would share in His role as the principle of existence. Do you ever act as if some created thing—your job, your health, a relationship—has the power to be the ultimate cause of your happiness or success? What would it mean today to consciously trace every good thing back to God alone as the sole principle?
?
If this argument is true, then nothing in the universe has any independence in causing existence. How does that challenge your hidden assumptions about cause and effect in your daily life? Will you commit to reminding yourself, 'There is no creator but Allah, no sustainer but Allah' every time you catch yourself attributing ultimate causality to something else?
?
This lesson shows that sharing in God's principality is absurd. But is there any area of your life where you have mentally elevated a person, an object, or even your own efforts to a level of 'lordship' over you? What is one concrete step you can take this week to dethrone that false lord and reaffirm God's exclusive principality?

Second Absurdity: Negation as Part of the Cause
Razi presents the second horn and an objection with response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why making the negation part of the principle of contingents is impossible and how it undermines the cosmological argument.

Core Idea
If negation is part of the cause of being, then non-being could cause being, destroying the proof for God.

Razi now considers the second horn of the dilemma: that the principle of contingents includes the negative qualification. He argues this leads to an impossibility: non-being would be part of the cause of being. Moreover, it would make the cosmological argument invalid. The lesson then examines an objection and Razi's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
From 'وإن كانت المبدئية' to the end. Note the structure: second horn, implication, objection, response.
2
Understand the impossibility
Razi argues that if negation is part of the cause of being, then non-being is part of the cause, which is impossible.
A cause must have reality; non-being cannot be a cause.
3
Grasp the implication
If non-being can be part of a cause, it could be the entire cause, which would invalidate the proof from contingency.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objector suggests that the pure existence entails an attribute that is the principle. Razi responds by applying the same division.
The entailed attribute would be either without or with the negation, repeating the problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على فساد هذا المذهب : أنه لو لم تكن للباري تعالى ماهية وحقيقة إلا الوجود المقيد بالقيد السلبي وهو أنه غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات فمبدأ وجودات الممكنات إما أن يكون هو ذلك الوجود لا بشركة من ذلك السلب وإما أن يكون بشركة من ذلك السلب، فإن المبدأ هو ذلك الوجود لا بمشاركة من ذلك السلب وجب أن يكون أخر الموجودات مشاركًا لذات الحق سبحانه وتعالى في تلك المبدئية، وإن كانت المبدئية بمشاركة من ذلك السلب كان السلب جزءا من مبدأ الثبوت، وذلك محال ؛ إذ لو جاز في العقل أن يكون العدم جزءا لعلة الثبوت فليتجه أيضًا أن يكون تمام علة الثبوت، وحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نستدل بوجود الممكنات على وجود واجب الوجود. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الوجود المجرد مستلزما لصفة ويكون تلك الصفة مبدأ للممكنات؟ الوجود مع أن قلنا : التقسيم المذكور عائد في كيفية استلزام الوجود لتلك الصفة، وهو المؤثر في ذلك الاستلزام إما الوجود لا بمشاركة ذلك السلب أو بمشاركة ذلك السلب.
The second proof on the corruption of this doctrine: That if the Almighty has no essence and reality except existence restricted by a negative restriction—that it is not accidental to any essence—then the principle of the existences of contingents is either that existence without participation of that negation, or with participation of that negation. If the principle is that existence without participation of that negation, then other existents must share with the essence of the Truth, Exalted, in that principality. And if the principality is with participation of that negation, then negation becomes part of the principle of affirmation, and that is impossible; for if it were rationally possible for non-being to be part of the cause of being, then it would also be possible for it to be the entire cause of being. And then we cannot infer from the existence of contingents the existence of the Necessary Being. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that that pure existence entails a attribute and that attribute is the principle of contingents? (We say:) The aforementioned division returns concerning how existence entails that attribute, and the effective factor in that entailment is either existence without participation of that negation or with participation of that negation.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the view that God's essence is just existence with the negative qualification of being non-accidental. Razi considers the principle of contingent existences: if it is that existence without the negation, then other existents would share in principality (absurd). If the negation is part of the principle, then non-being becomes part of the cause of being, which is rationally impossible and would undermine the cosmological argument. An objection is raised: perhaps the pure existence entails an attribute which is the principle. Razi's answer: the same division applies—the entailment must be either with or without the negation, repeating the problem.

Key Terms

عدة
علة
استلزام
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negation can be part of the cause of existence as a conceptual component.
Correction: Negation is non-being and cannot be a real cause of existence; the cause must be positive.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if negation were part of the cause of being?
A) The cause would be perfect.
B) The proof for God's existence would become invalid.
C) God would have an essence.
D) The cause would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Including negation in the principle of contingents is impossible and would invalidate the cosmological argument.

?

The lesson says if non-being were part of the cause, the proof of God fails. Have you ever allowed yourself to think that something from non-existence could bring about good in your life without Allah? How does this argument purify your tawheed?

Razi's Third Proof: Existence Known, Essence Unknown
Razi argues that since existence is known but God's essence is unknown, they differ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that God's reality is distinct from existence, based on the knownness of existence and unknownness of God's essence, including the objection about negative restrictions and its refutation.

Core Idea
God's reality cannot be existence itself because existence is known but God is unknown.

In this proof, Razi appeals to a philosophical consensus to argue that existence is self-evident while God's inner reality is not. He then anticipates an objection that the unknown part might be negative restrictions, and refutes it. The passage concludes by noting that only negations and relations are known about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the two premises: existence is self-evident, and God's essence is unknown. Then see how the conclusion follows: therefore God's reality is not existence.
3
Examine the objection and reply
The objection suggests the unknown part might be negative restrictions. Razi replies that negative restrictions are known, so they cannot be the unknown element.
Think about why he says negative restrictions are known: we can conceive that His existence is not accidental.
4
Reflect on the final point
Razi notes that philosophers agree only negations and relations are known about God. Consider how this supports the distinction between essence and existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : اتفق الحكماء على أن الوجود بديهي التصور، والدلائل العقلية ناطقة بذلك، واتفق الحكماء على أن كنه ماهية الحق سبحانه غير معقول للبشر، والبراهين العقلية ناطقة بذلك، وإذا كان الوجود معلوم التصور وحقيقة الحق سبحانه وتعالى غير معلومة التصور وجب أن تكون حقيقة الحق سبحانه غير الوجود. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المجهول من حقيقة الحق قيوده السلبية ؟ قلنا : هذا باطل لأن القيود السلبية ،معلومة ولذلك فإنا يمكننا أن نعقل أن وجوده غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات أصلا. واتفقت الفلاسفة على أن المعلوم من الحق سبحانه هو السلوب والإضافات.
The third proof: The philosophers agree that existence is self-evident in conception, and rational proofs indicate that. And the philosophers agree that the inner reality of the essence of the Truth, Exalted, is not intelligible to humans, and rational proofs indicate that. And if existence is known in conception and the reality of the Truth, Exalted, is not known in conception, then it must be that the reality of the Truth, Exalted, is other than existence. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that the unknown aspect of the reality of the Truth is its negative restrictions? We say: This is false because negative restrictions are known, and therefore we can conceive that His existence is not accidental to any essence at all. And the philosophers agree that what is known of the Truth, Exalted, is the negations and relations.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. Razi uses a consensus of philosophers: existence is self-evident, but God's inmost essence is unknown. Since the known and unknown cannot be identical, God's reality must be other than existence. An objection suggests that the unknown part might be negative restrictions. Razi replies that negative restrictions are known (e.g., we understand that His existence is not accidental), so they cannot be the unknown part. He then notes that philosophers agree that only negations and relations are known about God.

Key Terms

تصور
كنه
ماهية
قيد سلبي

إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God has any negative attributes.
Correction: The argument does not deny negative attributes; it denies that God's essence is merely existence with negations. Negative attributes are in fact known and do not account for the unknownness of God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why can't God's reality be existence itself?
A) Existence is not a real attribute.
B) Existence is self-evident but God's essence is unknown, so they must be distinct.
C) God's existence is accidental to His essence.
D) The philosophers disagree on this point.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that God's essence is distinct from existence by showing that existence is known while God's essence is unknown, and that negative restrictions do not explain this unknownness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If existence is self-evident but God's essence is not, how does that change the way you approach the question 'Who is God?' Are you content to know Him through His attributes and actions, or do you secretly wish to penetrate His essence? How can you cultivate contentment with what He has revealed?

Necessity Implies Distinction of Essence and Existence
Razi proves the distinction between essence and existence using logical analysis of predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that necessity, being a quality of a relation, requires that God's essence is distinct from His existence.

Core Idea
Necessity is a quality of a relation, and a relation needs two distinct terms.

This lesson presents Razi's fourth proof, which uses a logical analysis of predication to show that necessity implies a distinction between essence and existence. Razi argues that when we say something 'must be,' the necessity qualifies the relation between subject and predicate, not the subject itself. Applying this to God, for 'necessary existence' to be meaningful, God's reality must be distinct from His existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the argument. Then read the full translation. Notice how Razi builds from a general logical principle to a specific application to God.
Pay attention to the example of 'man must be an animal' – it illustrates the logical structure.
2
Understand the logical framework
Identify the three components: subject (موضوع), predicate (محمول), and the relation (نسبة/رابطة) between them. Then understand that necessity (وجوب) is a quality (كيفية) of this relation.
This comes from the science of logic (علم المنطق).
3
Apply the framework to God
Replace the example with 'The Creator must be existent.' Here the subject is the Creator's reality (حقيقته), the predicate is existence, and the relation is the attribution of existence to that reality. Necessity qualifies this relation.
The subject is not 'God' in the sense of His essence, but His reality (حقيقته).
4
Grasp the conclusion
For the necessity of the relation to be intelligible, the two relata (subject and predicate) must be distinct. Otherwise, necessity would be a quality of an identity, which is meaningless. Therefore, God's reality is other than His existence.
This does not deny God's necessary existence; it shows that necessity itself implies distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : ثبت في علم المنطق أن الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان كيفيات لنسب المحمولات إلى الموضوعات مثلاً إذا قلنا : " الإنسان يجب أن يكون حيوانا. فالإنسان هو الموضوع، والحيوان هو المحمول، وثبوت الحيوان للإنسان هو النسبة، المسماة بالرابطة، ثم هذه النسبة موصوفة بالوجوب، وهذا الوجوب كيفية لهذه النسبة، وهي كلام حق معقول. إذا عرفت هذا فن قول : إذا قلنا : " يجب أن يكون الباري تعالى موجوداً فالباري هو الموضوع ، والموجود هو المحمول، وإسناد الموجود إلى تلك الحقيقة هو الرابطة، والوجوب كيفية لهذه النسبة ،والرابطة وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يصر إثبات وجوب الوجود في حق الباري تعالى معقولاً ، إلا إذا قلنا إن حقيقته مغايرة لوجوده.
The fourth proof: It is established in the science of logic that necessity, impossibility, and possibility are qualities of the relations of predicates to subjects. For example, when we say: 'Man must be an animal.' Man is the subject, animal is the predicate, and the affirmation of animal for man is the relation, called the copula. Then this relation is described by necessity, and this necessity is a quality of this relation, and this is a true and intelligible statement. When you have understood this, then our statement: When we say: 'The Exalted Creator must be existent', then the Creator is the subject, the existent is the predicate, and the attribution of existent to that reality is the copula, and necessity is a quality of this relation and the copula. And if the matter is so, the affirmation of the necessity of existence for the Exalted Creator does not become intelligible unless we say that His reality is other than His existence.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof based on logic. Razi explains that necessity, impossibility, and possibility are qualities of the relation (copula) between subject and predicate. For example, in 'Man must be an animal,' the necessity qualifies the relation of animal to man. Applying this to 'The Creator must be existent,' the necessity qualifies the relation of existence to the Creator's reality. For this to be intelligible, the subject (Creator's reality) and predicate (existence) must be distinct; otherwise, the necessity would be a quality of an identity, which is not meaningful. Thus God's reality is other than His existence.

Key Terms
وجوب
امتناع
إمكان
نسبة
رابطة
كيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that this proof denies God's necessary existence or that it claims existence is accidental to God.
Correction: The proof actually affirms that God's existence is necessary, but it uses the logical analysis of necessity as a quality of a relation to argue that necessity itself requires a distinction between the reality (essence) and existence. It does not deny the necessity; it explains its logical structure.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why must God's reality be other than His existence?
A) Because existence is a predicate in logic.
B) Because necessity is a quality of a relation, and that requires two distinct terms.
C) Because God cannot be described as necessary.
D) Because logic only applies to creatures.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the logical concept of necessity as a quality of a relation entails that in God, essence and existence are distinct, not identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is necessary', do you truly grasp that necessity is not a property of His essence alone but of the relation between His essence and existence? How does this refine your understanding of His absolute self-sufficiency?
?
This proof forces you to distinguish between what Allah is (essence) and that He is (existence). Are you content to simply affirm His existence, or does this distinction drive you to seek deeper knowledge of His attributes? What practical step will you take this week to contemplate the meaning of Allah's necessity?
?
If Allah's essence and existence are distinct, then your own existence is a gift entirely from Him. Does your daily life reflect this dependence? Or do you live as if existence is something you own? What will you change today to acknowledge that your existence is a relation to the Necessary Being?

Razi's Counter-Argument: Applying Ibn Sina's Logic to God
Razi turns Ibn Sina's argument against him to show the distinction applies to God as well.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Ibn Sina's argument for the distinction of essence and existence in contingents to prove the same distinction in God.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's own argument proves too much — it applies to God as well.

In this passage, Razi presents a clever counter-proof against Ibn Sina's position. Rather than rejecting Ibn Sina's reasoning outright, he shows that the very argument Ibn Sina used to distinguish essence from existence in contingent beings can be applied to God, leading to a conclusion Ibn Sina would reject. This demonstrates that the argument is either invalid or proves more than intended.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the text is incomplete — it cuts off mid-sentence. This is a known issue with the manuscript.
2
Understand Ibn Sina's original argument
Ibn Sina argued that we can conceive the essence of a contingent thing (e.g., a horse) while doubting its existence. Once we know it exists, we realize the essence and existence are distinct. This shows that existence is additional to essence in contingents.
This is the premise Razi builds on.
3
See Razi's application to God
Razi says: 'Likewise here, we can conceive what the God of the world is and what the Maker of contingents is while we doubt...' The argument is cut off, but the intended conclusion is clear: if we can conceive God's essence while doubting His existence, then His essence and existence must also be distinct.
This turns Ibn Sina's own method against him.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
This is not a rejection of Ibn Sina's argument but a demonstration that it proves more than its author intended. If the argument works for contingents, it must also work for God — which forces Ibn Sina to either accept the distinction in God or find a flaw in his original argument.
This is a classic dialectical move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : احتج أبو علي بن سينا على أن وجود الممكنات مغاير لماهياتها بأن قال : يمكننا أن نعقل هذه الماهيات عندما نشك في وجودها إلى أن يقوم البرهان على كونها موجودة والمعلوم غير ما هو غير ،معلوم فماهياتها مغايرة لوجوداتها، فكذا ههنا يمكننا أن نعقل أن إله العالم ما هو وموجد الممكنات ما هو حال ما نشك في
The fifth proof: Abu Ali ibn Sina argued that the existence of contingents is different from their essences by saying: We can conceive these essences when we doubt their existence until a proof establishes that they exist, and what is known is not what is known. So their essences are different from their existences. Likewise here, we can conceive what the God of the world is and what the Maker of contingents is while we doubt...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, which is a counter-argument using Ibn Sina's own reasoning. Ibn Sina argued that in contingents, we can conceive their essences while doubting their existence, proving essence and existence are distinct. Razi applies the same logic to God: we can conceive God's essence while doubting His existence, so His essence must be distinct from His existence. The text is cut off, but the intended conclusion is clear: the same argument that Ibn Sina used for contingents works for God.

Key Terms
ممكن
ماهية
وجود
شك
برهان
إله العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi rejects Ibn Sina's argument entirely.
Correction: Razi does not reject the argument; he shows that it proves more than Ibn Sina intended — that it also applies to God, which Ibn Sina would deny.

Checkpoint
How does Razi use Ibn Sina's own argument against him?
A) He denies that we can conceive essences without existence.
B) He applies the same reasoning to God to show distinction of essence and existence.
C) He argues that doubt is impossible regarding God.
D) He claims the argument only works for contingents.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses Ibn Sina's own argument from conceiving essences while doubting existence to argue that God's essence and existence must be distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Razi applies Ibn Sina's logic consistently to God. Are you willing to follow your own reasoning to its honest conclusion, even if it challenges your current beliefs — or do you stop short when it becomes uncomfortable?

Objection and Answer: Can Pure Existence Entail a Quality?
Razi answers an objection about pure existence entailing a quality for possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument about whether pure existence alone can entail a quality that serves as the source of possibles, and how the answer invokes a previous distinction about the role of negation.

Core Idea
Existence alone does not automatically produce a quality; negation must be considered.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a subtle objection: if we say that pure existence (al-wujud al-mujarrad) cannot be the source of possibles, why not allow that it might entail a quality which then becomes that source? The answer is not a simple denial but a reference back to a previously established division concerning how existence relates to attributes. Understanding this exchange is key to grasping how kalam thinkers analyze the relationship between divine existence and its effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note what the objector is proposing: that pure existence might entail a quality, and that quality could be the source of possibles.
The objection challenges a previous claim that pure existence cannot be the source.
2
Identify the answer's structure
The answer does not directly accept or reject. Instead, it refers to a previous division (التقسيم المذكور) and says the effective factor in the entailment is either existence alone or existence with the participation of a negation.
The term 'السلب' (negation) is crucial.
3
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: why does the answer bring up negation? How does this connect to the earlier division? This shows that the quality cannot be entailed by pure existence alone; existence must be considered in relation to something else.
This demonstrates the methodical nature of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الوجود المجرد مستلزما لصفة وتكون تلك الصفة مبدأ للممكنات؟ الوجود مع أن قلنا : التقسيم المذكور عائد في كيفية استلزام الوجود لتلك الصفة، وهو المؤثر في ذلك الاستلزام إما الوجود لا بمشاركة ذلك السلب أو بمشاركة ذلك السلب.
For it was said: Why is it not permissible that that pure existence entail a quality and that quality be the source of possibles? The existence with that [؟] We said: The aforementioned division returns to the manner of the entailment of existence for that quality, and the effective factor in that entailment is either existence without participation of that negation or with participation of that negation.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer. The objector questions why pure existence cannot itself entail a quality that then becomes the source of possibles. The answer refers back to a previous division (التقسيم المذكور) and states that the effective factor in the entailment is either existence alone or existence with the participation of a negation (سلب). This is a technical distinction in kalam about how existence relates to attributes.

Key Terms
الوجود المجرد
صفة
مبدأ
الممكنات
استلزام
السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pure existence alone suffices to produce possibles.
Correction: The argument shows that pure existence cannot be the source unless a quality is entailed, and the entailment itself depends on whether negation participates, so pure existence alone is not sufficient.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the objection, the effective factor in the entailment of the quality is either existence alone or existence with participation of what?
A) The quality itself
B) The negation (السلب)
C) The possible
D) The essence

Key Takeaway
The entailment of a quality from pure existence is not straightforward; it depends on a prior conceptual distinction involving negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection assumes that pure existence could entail a quality. In your daily life, do you ever assume that your mere existence as a Muslim (by label) automatically entails the quality of taqwa or sincerity? What does this passage teach you about the need for an intentional distinction (like negation) to actually produce that quality?
?
The effective factor in the entailment is either existence alone or existence with negation. Reflect on a specific relationship or responsibility you have. Is your 'existence' in that role enough, or is there a 'negation' (something you must let go of or avoid) that is necessary for a meaningful outcome? Describe it.
?
Razi's answer relies on a previously established division. How often do you base your understandings on forgotten prior distinctions? What is one prior distinction in your Islamic knowledge that you tend to overlook, leading to wrong conclusions about your faith or practice? Commit to revisiting it this week.

Third Proof: Existence Known, Essence Unknown
Razi's third proof: existence known, essence unknown, therefore distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi proves that God's inner reality is not existence, based on the contrast between what is self-evident and what is incomprehensible.

Core Idea
Existence is known, God's essence is unknown — they cannot be identical.

This lesson presents Razi's third proof, which uses a simple but powerful logical contrast: existence is self-evident to all, yet God's true essence is beyond human comprehension. From this, Razi concludes that God's reality must be other than existence. The passage also addresses an objection that the unknown might be merely negative attributes, and Razi refutes it by affirming that negatives are known.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two premises
The proof relies on two agreed-upon facts: (1) existence is self-evident (بديهي التصور), and (2) the inner reality of God's essence (كنه ماهية الحق) is unintelligible to humans. Both are supported by philosophers and intellectual proofs.
Take note of the term 'بديهي التصور' — it means the concept is so clear it needs no definition.
2
See the logical conclusion
Since existence is known and God's essence is unknown, they cannot be the same thing. Therefore, God's reality (حقيقة الحق) is not existence.
This is a straightforward argument from difference in knowability.
3
Examine the objection and its refutation
An opponent might say: 'Perhaps what is unknown about God are just negative qualifications (قيود سلبية).' Razi rejects this because negative qualifications are themselves known — we can conceive that God's existence is not attached to any essence. So they cannot be the unknown part.
The passage concludes that only negations (سلوب) and relations (إضافات) are known of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : اتفق الحكماء على أن الوجود بديهي التصور، والدلائل العقلية ناطقة بذلك، واتفق الحكماء على أن كنه ماهية الحق سبحانه غير معقول للبشر، والبراهين العقلية ناطقة بذلك، وإذا كان الوجود معلوم التصور وحقيقة الحق سبحانه وتعالى غير معلومة التصور وجب أن تكون حقيقة الحق سبحانه غير الوجود. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المجهول من حقيقة الحق قيوده السلبية ؟ قلنا : هذا باطل لأن القيود السلبية ،معلومة ولذلك فإنا يمكننا أن نعقل أن وجوده غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات أصلا. واتفقت الفلاسفة على أن المعلوم من الحق سبحانه هو السلوب والإضافات.
The third proof: The philosophers agreed that existence is self-evident in conception, and intellectual proofs testify to that. And the philosophers agreed that the inner reality of the essence of the Truth (may He be exalted) is unintelligible to humans, and intellectual proofs testify to that. And since existence is known in conception and the reality of the Truth (may He be exalted and hallowed) is unknown in conception, it must be that the reality of the Truth (may He be exalted) is other than existence. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that what is unknown of the reality of the Truth be its negative qualifications? We said: This is false because negative qualifications are known, and therefore we can conceive that His existence does not attach to any essence at all. And the philosophers agreed that what is known of the Truth (may He be exalted) is the negations and relations.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة الثالثة) and an answer to an objection. The proof begins by citing two agreements among philosophers (الحكماء): (1) existence is self-evident in conception, and (2) the inner reality (كنه) of God's essence is incomprehensible. From these, it follows that God's reality must be different from existence. An objection is raised: the unknown aspects might be negative attributes. The refutation is that negative attributes are known, so they cannot be the unknown part. The passage concludes that only negations (سلوب) and relations (إضافات) are known of God.

Key Terms
بديهي التصور
كنه ماهية الحق
غير معقول
قيود سلبية
السلوب والإضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unknown aspects of God are negative attributes (قيود سلبية), so they are what is incomprehensible.
Correction: Negative attributes are actually known and conceivable; what is incomprehensible is God's positive inner reality (كنه).

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why must God's reality be other than existence?
A) Existence is unknown but God's essence is known
B) Existence is self-evident but God's essence is incomprehensible
C) Both existence and God's essence are unknown
D) The philosophers disagreed about existence

Key Takeaway
Existence is self-evident, God's essence is unknowable — therefore they are distinct; only negations and relations are known of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi argues that God's essence is unknown, yet we can know Him through negations and relations. Have you ever felt frustrated by not being able to fully comprehend God? How does knowing that even the philosophers agreed on this limitation affect your humility in worship?
?
This proof distinguishes between existence (which is self-evident) and essence (which is unknown). In your daily life, do you tend to treat your own existence and essence as the same? How might acknowledging that difference in God change the way you approach prayer or trust in Him?

Fifth Proof: Ibn Sina on Conceivability of Essence
Razi presents Ibn Sina's argument that essence is conceivable without existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that the conceivability of essence without existence proves that essence is distinct from existence, and how this reasoning extends to the divine essence.

Core Idea
Essence can be conceived without existence — so they must be distinct.

Ibn Sina's fifth proof is a powerful argument based on what we can conceive. He argues that if we can think of an essence while doubting whether it exists, then that essence cannot be identical to its existence. This reasoning is then applied to the divine essence. Let's examine the argument as Razi presents it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: premise, reasoning, conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
The key premise is that we can conceive of essences while doubting their existence. Identify how Ibn Sina uses this to assert that essence and existence are distinct for possibles.
3
Apply the same reasoning to God
Ibn Sina then applies the same logic to God. See how the argument moves from possibles to the Creator.
This is a crucial step in the proof.
4
Note the subtle response
Razi ends by noting that Ibn Sina himself could answer this argument with a subtle difference. Consider what that might be.
This shows that even the proponent can see a potential flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : احتج أبو علي بن سينا على أن وجود الممكنات مغاير لماهياتها بأن قال : يمكننا أن نعقل هذه الماهيات عندما نشك في وجودها إلى أن يقوم البرهان على كونها موجودة والمعلوم غير ما هو غير ،معلوم فماهياتها مغايرة لوجوداتها، فكذا ههنا يمكننا أن نعقل أن إله العالم ما هو وموجد الممكنات ما هو حال ما نشك في ، وجوده إلى أن يثبت بالبرهان كونه موجودًا، والمعلوم غير ما هو غير معلوم، فهذا يقتضي أن تكون حقيقته غير وجوده. واعلم أنه يمكنه أن يجيب عن هذا الوجه بفرق لطيف.
The fifth proof: Abu Ali ibn Sina argued that the existences of possibles are distinct from their essences by saying: We can conceive of these essences when we doubt their existence until a proof is established that they exist. The known is other than the unknown. So their essences are distinct from their existences. Likewise here, we can conceive of what the God of the world is and what the Bringer of possibles into existence is in the state when we doubt His existence until it is established by proof that He exists. The known is other than the unknown. So this requires that His reality be other than His existence. And know that he (Ibn Sina) could answer this argument with a subtle difference.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (الحجة الخامسة) attributed to Ibn Sina. The proof argues that since we can conceive of the essences of possibles while doubting their existence, essence is distinct from existence. The same applies to God: we can conceive of what God is (إله العالم ما هو) while doubting His existence, so His reality is different from His existence. The author notes that Ibn Sina himself could respond to this argument with a subtle distinction (فرق لطيف), indicating awareness of potential objections.

Key Terms
مغاير
الماهيات
إله العالم
موجد الممكنات
فرق لطيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If essence is conceivable without existence, then existence is merely accidental to essence.
Correction: The argument only shows that essence is distinct from existence, not that existence is accidental. Ibn Sina's own subtle distinction (فرق لطيف) suggests a nuance that preserves the necessary connection for God.

Checkpoint

A) We cannot conceive of God at all
B) We can conceive of God's essence while doubting His existence
C) Existence is self-evident
D) The philosophers agreed

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina argues that because we can conceive of an essence without its existence, essence and existence must be distinct, and this applies even to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Uniformity of Species Argument
Razi uses species uniformity to argue against God as pure existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the premise of species uniformity to argue that God cannot be defined as pure existence.

Core Idea
If God were pure existence, all things would share His properties — a contradiction.

Razi presents his sixth proof, which relies on a widely accepted philosophical premise: all individuals of a species share all true properties. By applying this to the concept of pure existence as the reality of God, he shows the absurd conclusions that follow, thereby refuting the notion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: premise, application, consequences, conclusion.
The passage is concise and argumentative.
2
Identify the premise
Find the premise accepted by Ibn Sina and most rational people: that individuals of the ultimate species share all true properties.
This is the fundament of the argument.
3
Apply the premise to pure existence
Razi applies this premise to the hypothesis that God's reality is pure existence. What follows if everything true of God is true of all existents?
Think about entailments (لوازم الذات).
4
Notice the contradictions
The argument yields two contradictory results: contingent beings would become necessary, and God would become contingent. Can you see both?
These contradictions show the original assumption is false.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the assumption leads to contradiction and is therefore false. What does this tell you about the nature of God?
This proof refutes the idea that God is pure existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة الحجة السادسة ههنا ،مقدمة سلم أبو علي بن سينا وأكثر العقلاء صحتها واستقامتها، وهي أن أفراد النوع الأخير كل ما صح على واحد منها صح على كلها وقد بنى أبو علي على هذه المقدمة في كتبه الحكمية مطالب كثيرة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لو كانت حقيقة الباري تعالى هي محض الوجود كان كل ما كان من لوازم ذاته وجب أن يكون حاصلا لجميع الموجودات، وإن كان وجوده أخص الموجودات كل ما كان ممتنعا على ذاته وجب أن يكون ممتنعا على سائر الموجودات، وهو يفضي إلى التناقض؛ لأنه كما أن وجود هذه المحدثات للكائنات الفاسدات وجود ضعيف سريع الزوال والعدم، وجب أن يكون وجود الحق كذلك، وكما أن وجود الحق واجب الدوام ممتنع التغير وجب أن تكون هذه الموجودات الحسية كذلك، وذلك يفضي إلى التناقض، وكل ذلك باطل.
The sixth question. The sixth proof: Here there is a premise whose correctness and soundness Ibn Sina and most rational people have accepted, namely that the individuals of the ultimate species: everything that is true of one of them is true of all of them. Ibn Sina has built many claims upon this premise in his philosophical books. When you know this, we say: If the reality of the Creator (may He be exalted) were pure existence, then everything that is among the entailments of His essence would necessarily be found in all existents. And if His existence is the most specific of existents, everything that is impossible for His essence would necessarily be impossible for other existents. This leads to contradiction. Because just as the existence of these originated, corruptible beings is weak existence, quickly passing and perishing, then the existence of the Truth would have to be like that. And just as the existence of the Truth is necessarily perpetual, impossible to change, then these sensory existents would have to be like that. This leads to contradiction, and all that is false.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is the sixth proof (الحجة السادسة) within the sixth question (المسألة السادسة). It uses a premise accepted by Ibn Sina and most rational people: all individuals of the ultimate species (أفراد النوع الأخير) share all properties. If God's reality were pure existence, then all the entailments of His essence (لوازم ذاته) would apply to all existents, and impossibilities would also apply universally. This leads to contradictory consequences: contingent beings would have necessary existence, and God would have contingent properties. Such contradictions render the assumption false.

Key Terms
النوع الأخير
أفراد
لوازم الذات
محض الوجود
التناقض
المحدثات
الكائنات الفاسدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The uniformity premise applies only to natural species, not to metaphysical concepts like existence.
Correction: Razi applies it to existence as a genus, arguing that if God's reality is pure existence, then all existents share that property, leading to contradictions.

Checkpoint
The sixth proof argues that if God were pure existence, then all existents would share His necessary entailments. What premise supports this?
A) All philosophers agree on existence
B) Necessity is a quality of the copula
C) Individuals of the ultimate species share all true properties
D) Essence is conceivable without existence

Key Takeaway
Razi's sixth proof shows that defining God as pure existence leads to absurd contradictions, thus such a definition must be false.

Ibn Sina's Objection and Razi's Response on Accidental Existence
Razi rebuts Ibn Sina's objection that existence cannot be accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a parity argument to neutralize Ibn Sina's objection that God's existence cannot be accidental.

Core Idea
The same priority problem that Ibn Sina raises applies equally to the opponent's own view.

This passage presents a sophisticated theological debate between two giants of Islamic thought. Ibn Sina offers a logical objection against the Ash'ari view that God's existence is an accidental attribute; Razi responds by turning the logic back on Ibn Sina's own position about created beings. The exchange demonstrates the power of parity arguments in Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read Ibn Sina's objection (the first half of the passage) and note the logical steps: if existence were accidental, it would be dependent, hence possible, requiring a cause that leads to absurdity.
Ibn Sina's argument has two branches: the cause cannot be the essence itself nor another.
2
Study Razi's response
Read the second half (starting with 'The answer'). Razi flips the argument: the same priority issue applies to the recipient of existence in possibles, which everyone accepts.
Razi introduces the concept of 'recipient' (قابل) as parallel to 'efficient cause' (مؤثر).
3
Understand the parity argument
Notice how Razi concludes with a question: if you allow essences to precede their own existence by themselves, why not allow the same for the efficient cause? This neutralizes the objection.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum applied symmetrically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أبو علي بن سينا على صحة قوله بأن وجود الباري سبحانه وتعالى لو كان صفة عارضة لماهيته لكان مفتقرًا إلى تلك الماهية والمفتقر إلى الغير هو الممكن لذاته، فذلك الوجود ممكن لذاته، والممكن لذاته لا من مؤثر والمؤثر فيه إما تلك الماهية أو غير تلك الماهية لا جائز أن يكون المؤثر في ذلك الوجود تلك الماهية ؛ لأن المؤثر متقدم بالوجود على الأثر فيلزم أن تكون تلك الماهية متقدمة بوجودها على وجودها، وذلك محال. ولا جائز أن يكون المؤثر في ذلك الوجود غير تلك الماهية؛ لأنه يلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته مفتقرًا في وجوده إلى سبب منفصل، وذلك محال. والجواب كما أن المؤثر متقدم بالوجود على الأثر فكذلك القابل متقدم بالوجود على المقبول، ولا نزاع أن وجود الممكنات زائد على ماهياتها، فيلزم أن تكون تلك الماهيات القابلة لتلك الموجودات متقدمة بوجودها على وجودها، وذلك محال. وإن قلتم : لم لا يجوز أن تكون هذه الماهيات متقدمة على وجوداتها بنفس تلك الماهيات لا بوجود آخر ؟ قلنا : فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال كذلك في جانب المؤثر ؟
And Abu Ali ibn Sina argued for the correctness of his statement that if the existence of the Creator (may He be exalted and hallowed) were an accidental attribute to His essence, it would be dependent on that essence, and the dependent on another is possible in itself. So that existence is possible in itself, and what is possible in itself requires an efficient cause. The efficient cause is either that essence or other than that essence. It is not permissible that the efficient cause of that existence be that essence, because the efficient cause precedes the effect in existence, so it would require that that essence precedes its own existence by its existence, and that is impossible. And it is not permissible that the efficient cause of that existence be other than that essence, because it would require that the Necessary in itself be dependent in its existence on a separate cause, and that is impossible. The answer: Just as the efficient cause precedes the effect in existence, so too the recipient precedes the received in existence. There is no dispute that the existences of possibles are additional to their essences. So it would require that those essences which receive those existences precede their own existences by their existence, and that is impossible. And if you say: Why is it not permissible that these essences precede their existences by those very essences, not by another existence? We say: Then why is it not permissible for the situation to be the same on the side of the efficient cause?
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Ibn Sina and a response. Ibn Sina argues that if existence were an accident (عارضة) added to God's essence, it would be dependent on that essence, making it possible. Possibles require an efficient cause. The cause cannot be the essence itself (circular priority) nor another (compromising necessity). The response flips the argument: just as the efficient cause must precede the effect, the recipient (قابل) must precede the received (مقبول). Everyone agrees that possibles have existence additional to essences, so by the same reasoning, those essences would have to precede their own existence — an absurdity. The exchange concludes with a parity argument: if the opponent allows essences to precede their own existences by themselves, then the same should be allowed on the side of the efficient cause, neutralizing the objection.

Key Terms
صفة عارضة
مفتقر
الممكن لذاته
مؤثر
أثر
قابل
مقبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's objection definitively proves that existence cannot be accidental to God.
Correction: Razi shows that the same reasoning leads to an absurdity for Ibn Sina's own position on created beings, using a parity argument.

Checkpoint
In the response to Ibn Sina's objection, the author argues that the same problem of priority applies to what?
A) The efficient cause
B) The recipient (قابل) of existence
C) The essence of God
D) The possible itself

Key Takeaway
Razi's parity argument shows that Ibn Sina's objection is not decisive, as the same logical structure applies to created essences and their existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking of God as dependent on anything—your worship, your dua, or even His own attributes? How does this lesson's emphasis on God's self-sufficiency challenge that subtle assumption, and what will you do today to affirm His absolute independence in your heart?
?
If your own existence is entirely received from God at every moment, what does that say about your claim to any independent worth or achievement? How will you live today with the constant awareness that you are nothing but a recipient?
?
The parity argument shows that the same logical problem exists for created essences as for God—so why do you accept that you exist without falling into absurdity? What does your own existence teach you about God's power and your complete dependence on Him, and how will you turn that into gratitude right now?
