The Second Proof of Divine Unity: The Impossibility of Conflicting Wills
Razi enumerates three possibilities for two gods with conflicting wills and explains their impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof for God's oneness, which shows that two gods with conflicting wills would lead to absurdity.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot exist because their wills would conflict, leading to impossibility or impotence.

This lesson examines the second of Razi's proofs for tawhid, based on the nature of divine power. If two gods each willed opposite things, neither could achieve their will or only one could, but both scenarios are impossible. By analyzing this argument, you'll see how kalam uses logical reasoning to establish God's unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: Razi presents three possibilities and explains why each fails.
The passage is incomplete; the final conclusion is missing, but the reasoning is clear up to that point.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Razi considers three outcomes when two gods will opposites: both wills occur, neither occurs, or only one occurs. Find them in the text.
3
Understand the reasoning for impossibility
For each possibility, follow Razi's argument. The first is contradictory because both movement and rest cannot coexist. The second collapses into the first. The third is impossible due to impotence and equal capacity.
The second reason for the third possibility is explained in detail: the object of power is indivisible, so one power cannot be stronger.
4
Note the incomplete passage
The text cuts off during the third possibility. Based on the logic, what do you think the conclusion would be? Likely that the assumption of two gods leads to absurdity, proving oneness.
5
Reflect on the proof's significance
Consider how this logical argument supports the core Islamic belief in tawhid. It shows that reason alone can demonstrate the impossibility of multiple gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الإله هو الذي يكون قادرا على المقدورات. ولو فرضنا إلهين أراد أحدهما حركة زيد والآخر سكونه. فإما أن يحصل المرادان، وهو محال، أو لا يحصل مراد واحد منهما وذلك أيضا محال لأن المانع لكل واحد منهما عن حصول مراده ليس هو مجرد كون الآخر قادرًا، بل حصول مقدور الآخر. فإذن لا يمتنع حصول مراد هذا إلا إذا حصل مقدور ذاك ولا يمتنع حصول مراد ذاك إلا إذا حصل مقدور هذا . فلو حصل مراد كل واحد منهما لحصل مقدور كل واحد منهما. وحينئذ يرجع هذا القسم الثاني إلى القسم الأول. وقد بينا أنه محال . أو يحصل مراد أحدهما دون الآخر. وهذا أيضا محال لوجهين: الأول : أن الذي حصل مراده فهو قادر والذي لم يحصل مراده فهو عاجز، والعاجز لا يصلح للإلهية. والثاني : لما كانت لقدرة كل واحد منهما صلاحية التأثير في إيجاد ذلك المقدور. فنقول : إنه يستحيل أن يقال: إن إحدى هاتين الصلاحيتين أقوى الأخرى وذلك لأن المقدور شيء واحد لا يقبل القسمة أصلا وإذا كان كذلك، فهو إما أن من
The second proof: The god is one who is powerful over things that are possible. And if we suppose two gods, one willing Zayd's movement and the other his rest, then either both objects of will occur—and that is impossible—or neither object of will occurs—and that is also impossible, because the preventer for each of them from the occurrence of his object of will is not merely the other's being powerful, but rather the occurrence of the other's object of power. So the occurrence of this one's object of will is not prevented unless that one's object of power occurs, and the occurrence of that one's object of will is not prevented unless this one's object of power occurs. So if each one's object of will occurred, each one's object of power would occur. Then this second division reverts to the first division, and we have already shown that it is impossible. Or one's object of will occurs without the other. This too is impossible for two reasons: First, the one whose object of will occurred is powerful, and the one whose object of will did not occur is impotent, and the impotent is not fit for divinity. Second, since each one's power has the capacity to produce that object of power, we say: it is impossible to say that one of these two capacities is stronger than the other, because the object of power is a single thing that does not admit division at all. And if that is so, then it is either that... from
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is the second proof for God's oneness, based on the impossibility of two gods with conflicting wills. Razi begins by defining a god as one who is powerful (qadir) over possible things (maqdurat). He then presents a scenario: suppose two gods, one willing Zayd's movement and the other willing his rest. Three outcomes are possible: (1) both objects of will occur—impossible because a thing cannot be both moving and resting simultaneously; (2) neither occurs—but this reverts to the first case because each god's inability to achieve his will is not due to the other's mere power but to the actual occurrence of the other's object of power, so if neither occurs, it implies both objects of power (movement and rest) occur, contradiction; (3) only one god's will occurs. This is impossible for two reasons: the god whose will fails is impotent ('ajiz), and impotence is incompatible with divinity; and the two powers are equal in capacity over a single indivisible object, so neither can be stronger—thus it is impossible for one to prevail arbitrarily. The argument is cut off, but the conclusion is that the only coherent possibility is a single God.

Key Terms
قادر
المقدورات
المراد
العاجز
الصلاحية
لا يقبل القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Proofs for God's oneness rely solely on revelation, not reason.
Correction: Razi uses logical reasoning (a priori) to demonstrate that multiple gods lead to absurdity, showing that reason alone can establish tawhid.

Checkpoint
In Razi's second proof, why is the third possibility (one god's will occurring) impossible according to the second reason?
A) Because the god whose will fails would be impotent, which is unfit for divinity
B) Because the two powers are equal over an indivisible object, so one cannot be stronger
C) Because the two gods would have to agree
D) Because the object of power is divisible

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof demonstrates that logical consistency demands one divine will, hence only one God can exist.

If this proof is so clear, why do you find yourself sometimes acting as if there are multiple powers influencing your life—like fear of people or attachment to material things? How can you let this logical certainty transform your daily reliance on Allah alone?
?
The proof shows that any conflict of will leads to impotence. When you look at your own life, where do you see conflicting wills within yourself that make you spiritually impotent? How can you submit your will to the One God?

The First Proof for God's Oneness: The Argument from Composition
Razi presents a reductio ad absurdum proof for tawḥīd based on composition and possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that establishes the oneness of God by showing that the existence of two necessary beings leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Two necessary beings would imply composition, which contradicts necessary existence.

Imam Razi begins his proofs for God's oneness with a concise logical argument. This argument shows that if there were two necessary existents, they would have to be composite, but every composite is possible, not necessary — an impossibility. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its reliance on earlier discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
Notice the compact style: premise, reasoning, conclusion.
2
Identify the assumption and reasoning
Find the initial assumption (two necessary existents), the intermediate steps (sharing necessity, differing in individuation → composition), and the final conclusion (contradiction).
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Connect composition to possibility
Understand why every composite is possible by itself (depends on parts). Then see how this contradicts necessary existence.
This step relies on earlier discussion of composition in bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الحادية والعشرون
في بيان أن صانع العالم سبحانه وتعالى واحد
ويدل عليه وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : لو كان في الوجود شيئان كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود
لذاته لكانا مشتركين في الوجوب بالذات متباينين بالتعين فيلزم وقوع التركيب في ذات كل واحد منهما، وكل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته
ممكنا لذاته
وهو محال .
وتمام البحث فيه قد تقدم في مسألة حدوث الأجسام.
The twenty-first issue: On explaining that the Maker of the world, glory be to Him and exalted is He, is one. And there are proofs indicating it. The first proof: If there existed in existence two things each of which is necessary of existence by itself, they would share in necessity by essence and be distinct by individuation, so composition would follow in the essence of each of them. And every composite is possible by itself, so it would follow that the necessary by itself is possible by itself, and that is impossible. And the complete discussion of it has already preceded in the issue of the temporality of bodies.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the oneness of God (tawḥīd). It is a definition-and-proof passage that uses a reductio ad absurdum argument. The proof begins by assuming the existence of two necessary existents (wājib al-wujūd bi-dhātihā). Such two would share the attribute of necessary existence but differ in their individuation (taʿayyun). This difference implies composition (tarkīb) in each essence, because each would have a common attribute (necessity) and a distinguishing attribute (individuation). However, anything composite is possible (mumkin) by itself, since it depends on its parts. Therefore, the necessary being would be possible, which is a contradiction. Hence, two necessary existents cannot exist—God is one. The proof references an earlier discussion on the temporality of bodies (ḥudūth al-ajsām) for a fuller treatment of composition and possibility.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن لذاته
مركب
التعين
الوجوب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that composition is inherently impossible for God, so any composition shows that God cannot exist.
Correction: The argument does not assume composition is impossible for God; rather, it shows that composition entails possibility, which contradicts the very definition of necessary existence. The impossibility is in the contradiction, not in composition itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the first proof conclude that it is impossible for two necessary existents to exist?
A) Because they would necessarily disagree and cause conflict.
B) Because they would share in necessity but differ in individuation, leading to composition, which implies possibility.
C) Because each would be incapable of creating the other.
D) Because they would both be eternal and there is no room for two eternals.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for God's oneness shows that the existence of two necessary beings leads to a contradiction because they would be composite and hence possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that nothing can be both necessary and composite. Is there any created thing (a person, a possession, a status) that you treat as if it were necessary for your happiness? What would it take for you to recognize its possibility and release your attachment?
?
The proof begins with the assumption of two necessary beings and ends with contradiction. In your daily life, do you ever act as if there are two ultimate authorities—Allah and something else? What concrete step can you take today to remove that 'composition' from your heart and make Allah the sole necessary focus?
?
Razi uses a reductio: the impossibility of two necessary beings is proven by showing it leads to an absurdity. Are there areas of your faith where you hold contradictions (e.g., saying you rely on Allah but worrying excessively)? How can you apply the logical rigor of this proof to purify your own beliefs and actions?

Setting Up the Proof of Mutual Opposition
Razi introduces the scenario and analyzes the first two cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of two contradictory events occurring together or neither occurring disproves the existence of two gods.

Core Idea
Two gods willing opposite things leads to an unavoidable contradiction.

Imam Razi begins his second proof by establishing a simple but powerful scenario. If two gods existed and willed opposite things for the same object, we would face a logical dead end. This lesson explores the first two possible outcomes and shows why they are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the opening definition and the hypothetical scenario. Note how the author defines 'god' as omnipotent and sets up the conflict.
Focus on the logical structure: if both objects occur, that's a contradiction; if neither occurs, we get a circular prevention.
2
Understand the first case
The first case says both objects occur. This is impossible because motion and rest cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time.
3
Understand the second case
The second case says neither object occurs. The proof shows this collapses into the first case because the only prevention is the other's object occurring.
Notice the chain: if neither occurs, each object is prevented by the other's occurrence, but if neither occurs, then neither prevents—leading to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الإله هو الذي يكون قادرا على المقدورات. ولو فرضنا إلهين أراد أحدهما حركة زيد والآخر سكونه. فإما أن يحصل المرادان، وهو محال، أو لا يحصل مراد واحد منهما وذلك أيضا محال لأن المانع لكل واحد منهما عن حصول مراده ليس هو مجرد كون الآخر قادرًا، بل حصول مقدور الآخر. فإذن لا يمتنع حصول مراد هذا . إلا إذا حصل مقدور ذاك ولا يمتنع حصول مراد ذاك، إلا إذا حصل مقدور هذا . فلو حصل مراد كل واحد منهما لحصل مقدور كل واحد منهما. وحينئذ يرجع هذا القسم الثاني إلى القسم الأول. وقد بينا أنه محال . أو يحصل مراد أحدهما دون الآخر.
وهذا أيضا محال لوجهين
الأول : أن الذي حصل مراده. فهو قادر والذي لم يحصل مراده، فهو عاجز، والعاجز لا يصلح للإلهية.
والثاني : لما كانت لقدرة كل واحد منهما صلاحية التأثير في إيجاد ذلك المقدور. فنقول : إنه يستحيل أن يقال: إن إحدى هاتين الصلاحيتين أقوى الأخرى وذلك لأن المقدور شيء واحد لا يقبل القسمة أصلا وإذا كان كذلك، فهو إما أن يكون موجودا أو معدوما. فأما القسم الثالث. وهو أن يقال: إنه يصير بعضه موجودًا وبعضه معدوما . فذلك محال . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لم يكن إيجاد مثل هذا المقدور قابلا للتفاوت وإذا لم يقبل التفاوت امتنع كون إحدى القدرتين أقوى من الأخرى.
إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : لما حصل التساوي في قدرة هذين القادرين، فلو حصل أحدهما دون الثاني، لكان هذا ترجيحا لأحد المتساويين على الآخر، من غير مرجع البتة وذلك محال. فكان هذا القسم محالا . فثبت أن القول بوجود إلهين، يفضي إلى أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ولما ثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال باطل كان القول بوجود إلهين محالا باطلا.
The second proof: The god is the one who is powerful over all things that are objects of power (al-maqdūrāt). And if we suppose two gods, one wanting Zayd's motion and the other wanting his rest, then either both objects will occur—which is impossible—or neither of the two objects will occur—which is also impossible, because the preventer for each of them from the occurrence of his object is not merely the other's being powerful, but rather the occurrence of the other's object. So the occurrence of the object of this one is not prevented unless the object of that one occurs, and the occurrence of the object of that one is not prevented unless the object of this one occurs. Thus if the object of each were to occur, the object of each would occur, and then this second case reverts to the first case, which we have shown to be impossible—or [it means that] the object of one occurs without the other. This too is impossible for two reasons: First, the one whose object occurs is powerful (qādir), and the one whose object does not occur is incapable (ʿājiz), and the incapable is not fit for divinity. Second, since the power (qudra) of each has the capacity to bring about that object (al-maqdūr), we say: it is impossible to say that one of these two capacities is stronger than the other, because the object is a single thing which does not admit of division at all. And if that is so, then it is either existent or nonexistent. As for the third case—that it becomes partly existent and partly nonexistent—that is impossible. And if the matter is so, then bringing about such an object is not susceptible to variation, and if it is not susceptible to variation, it is impossible for one of the two powers to be stronger than the other. Once this is established, we say: since equality holds between the powers of these two powerful beings, if one [object] occurs without the other, this would be a preference of one equal over the other with no basis at all, and that is impossible. So this case is impossible. Thus it is established that the claim of the existence of two gods leads to one of these three cases, and since it is established that each of them is impossible and false, the claim of the existence of two gods is impossible and false.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction showing the impossibility of two gods (ilāhīn). It is an argument from mutual opposition (burhān al-tamānuʿ). The proof begins by defining God as omnipotent over all things (qādir ʿalā al-maqdūrāt). It then sets up a scenario: suppose two gods, one wills Zayd's motion, the other wills his rest. Three outcomes are possible: (1) both objects occur—impossible because they are contradictory; (2) neither object occurs—impossible because the only preventer would be the occurrence of the other's object, leading to a vicious circle; this case collapses into the first; (3) one object occurs without the other. This third case is also impossible for two reasons. First, the god whose object occurs is powerful, the other is incapable, and incapability disqualifies one from divinity. Second, since the object is unitary indivisible, the two powers are perfectly equal—neither can be stronger. If one object occurs rather than the other, it would be an arbitrary preference of equals with no basis, which is impossible. Thus all three possibilities are impossible, so the assumption of two gods is false.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes gods must disagree.
Correction: The proof shows that even the possibility of disagreement (given omnipotence) leads to contradiction; they need not actually disagree.

Checkpoint
Why does the second case (neither object occurs) revert to the first case?
A) Because if neither occurs, each god's object is prevented by the other's occurrence, but since neither occurs, no prevention happens, so both should occur.
B) Because the two gods would agree to cancel each other.
C) Because the objects are not real.
D) Because the scenario is impossible from the start.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of both or neither object occurring shows that the assumption of two gods with opposite wills leads to a logical dead end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your heart, do you ever act as if there are competing authorities in your life—people, desires, or fears—that have ultimate say over your actions? How does this proof challenge the idea that you can serve two masters and yet avoid contradiction?
?
If you truly believed that only One God has absolute power and will, would you still worry about the opinions of others as if they could override His decree? What would change in your daily decisions if you internalized the impossibility of any real opposition to Allah's will?
?
The proof shows that even the logical possibility of two gods is absurd. In your own life, when you find yourself torn between two competing commitments, do you seek to reconcile them or do you recognize that one must ultimately submit to the other? How does this argument expose the futility of trying to please both Allah and your own ego?
